    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="967e8b39f8e4449b849b8dc60cd06135">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '478894ba-d183-416c-b9cd-05401390815c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d72541011" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Here, the sodium storage mechanism in commercial grade hard carbon with a low surface area is comprehensively investigated using electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique, and in situ Raman spectroscopy for fresh and cycled electrodes. The reversible shift of the carbon G-band peak on Raman spectra and substantial change of the charge-transfer resistance in the sloping region of the voltage profile indicates the intercalation of sodium ions into hard carbon, whereas the low-voltage plateau is associated with the pore-filling process. In situ Raman analysis at low frequencies reveals that the pore filling is progressed via formation of small sodium clusters in closed pores. Prolonged cycling demonstrates that intercalation is stable and consistent throughout multiple charge–discharge cycles. The transition from intercalation to pore filling strongly affects the diffusion behavior, leading to considerably slower diffusivity at low voltage. The EIS effectively differentiates the contribution of adsorption to charge storage. The gradual growth of the solid-electrolyte interphase layer affects the rise of the interfacial resistance as cycling progresses. In combination with the slower diffusivity, the low-voltage plateau region strictly impedes fast de/sodiation and eventually causes capacity fade.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202304300-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202304300-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) as sodium is abundant and cost-effective compared to lithium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0001_R_d72541000e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0004_R_d72541000e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, metallic sodium is not suitable for use as the negative electrode for SIBs because of its unstable solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), high reactivity with water molecules in the electrolyte, and tendency of dendrite formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0005_R_d72541000e369" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Rev.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As an alternative, a host material for Na storage can be used as a negative electrode, provided that it has a low potential for desodiation. Unfortunately, graphite, a typical lithium-ion host, is not favorable for Na storage, although solvent co-intercalation may be used to achieve Na storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0006_R_d72541000e374" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">6</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0007_R_d72541000e374" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Environ. Sci.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Instead, hard carbon (HC) is a good option as the negative electrode for SIBs, with a sodium storage-specific capacity in the range of 200–480 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> below 1.2&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0008_R_d72541000e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0013_R_d72541000e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Hard carbon is a highly disordered material and also known as non-graphitizable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0010_R_d72541000e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Mater. Today">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbon is usually obtained by subjecting organic compounds to thermal treatment, a process called carbonization, at varying temperatures, leading to a complex structure of amorphous regions, micro- and nanopores, and sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon in pseudo-graphitic nano-domains.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0010_R_d72541000e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Mater. Today">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The latter can be considered graphenic-lamellae curved structures with highly defective layers. The uncertainty in understanding the hard carbon structure makes investigating its electrochemistry extremely challenging.</p>
         
         <p>Despite variations in preparation methods, microstructure, and porosity, it is interesting to note that hard carbon electrodes typically exhibit two distinct regions in the voltage profile: a sloping voltage profile above 100–200&nbsp;mV and a low-voltage plateau below.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0008_R_d72541000e406" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0012_R_d72541000e406" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Many works have proposed three distinct steps for Na storage: a) adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions on defects, b) intercalation/insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in pseudo-graphitic domains, and c) pore filling with sodium clusters or quasi-metallic sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0010_R_d72541000e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Mater. Today">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition to the reversible Na storage, formation of a SEI layer is inevitable for sodium storage. The SEI is usually composed of NaF, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, ROCONa, and RCH<sub>2</sub>ONa, which ranges from 10 to 80&nbsp;nm in thickness depending on the electrolyte composition and cycling conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0014_R_d72541000e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Front. Chem.">14</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0015_R_d72541000e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Different mechanisms can result from assigning the steps to a particular potential of Na storage. One of the first proposed mechanisms suggested that the sloping region of the voltage profile represents intercalation of sodium ions into pseudo-graphitic regions, followed by sodium insertion in micropores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0010_R_d72541000e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Mater. Today">10</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0016_R_d72541000e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">16</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0017_R_d72541000e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Alternatively, another mechanism suggested adsorption on defects at the sloping, higher voltage, and sodium intercalation at the plateau.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0018_R_d72541000e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The three-stage mechanisms include adsorption–intercalation–pore filling<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0019_R_d72541000e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Electrochim. Acta">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or defect adsorption–graphene layer adsorption–pore-filling processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0020_R_d72541000e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Energy Lett.">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0021_R_d72541000e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Solid-state <sup>23</sup>Na nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) studies have suggested that the storage of sodium between graphene layers is responsible for the sloping process, whereas the low-voltage plateau arises from small clusters rather than quasi-metallic sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0009_R_d72541000e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Power Sources">9</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0022_R_d72541000e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These studies also revealed an irreversible component of sodium that was attributed to SEI formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0022_R_d72541000e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Stratford et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0023_R_d72541000e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Commun.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0024_R_d72541000e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrated the formation of small Na clusters (13–15 Å) in the low-voltage region using NMR and Na storage above or between carbon layers with charge transfer to the carbon π* orbital during the sloping process.</p>
         
         <p>Not only spectroscopy but various electrochemical methods were used to study sodium storage dynamics in hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0019_R_d72541000e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Electrochim. Acta">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0025_R_d72541000e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">25</a>-<a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0028_R_d72541000e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Electrochim. Acta">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Schutjajew et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0025_R_d72541000e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrated that the sloping process is diffusion-dominated, while a constant response is obtained in the plateau region by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). In another study, de/sodiation of hard carbon was found to be a charge-transfer-controlled process, with nearly ten times slower kinetics compared to de/lithiation of hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0026_R_d72541000e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another work supported the intercalation process on the sloping region while reporting quasi-metallic sodium formation on the low-voltage plateau.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0028_R_d72541000e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Electrochim. Acta">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Reddy et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0020_R_d72541000e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Energy Lett.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Weaving et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0021_R_d72541000e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrated using in situ Raman measurements that the resulting G-band shift was obvious on the sloping voltage profile, which was regarded to be intercalation behavior of sodium ions. The Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation behavior on the sloping part was further emphasized by Tonnoir et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0029_R_d72541000e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Carbon">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and in a dilatometric study<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0030_R_d72541000e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> where a significant change in electrode thickness was measured on the sloping part for sodiation process. In addition, as a result of the mechano-electrochemical study by Yang et&nbsp;al.,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0031_R_d72541000e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Energy Chem.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the adsorption-intercalation behavior was assigned to the sloping region.</p>
         
         <p>As many hard-carbon studies were conducted using varying starting materials, porosity, and electrolytes, there is often controversy on the understanding of sodium storage into hard carbons and in comparing the results of different works. In this work, we combine in situ Raman spectroscopy, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), and transition electron microscopy (TEM) to comprehensively investigate the sodium storage in commercial grade hard carbon with a low specific surface area, 4.18 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, in terms of its kinetics and SEI formation during extensive cycling. We observed consistency between the in-depth EIS analysis and Raman studies, validating the two-step intercalation–pore-filling mechanism. No significant capacitive contribution was observed while intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions was responsible for the sodiation of hard carbon. In addition, gradual growth of the SEI layer upon multiple (de)sodiation led to an increase of the interfacial resistance so as to eventually hinder full sodiation at low voltage that causes capacity fading. In addition, we have rationalized several important parameters such as the activation energy, diffusion coefficient, and double-layer capacitance to discuss their role in the sodium storage mechanism and how they affect the electrochemical performance of commercial grade hard carbon with a low surface area.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202304300-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202304300-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) presents the basic physical properties of the hard carbon (HC) used in this work. The observed X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern shows typical disordered carbons showing a broad (≈ 7°FWHM) Bragg peak at ≈22.6° (2θ) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information). The particle linear size was estimated to be ≈8–12&nbsp;µm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information). The TEM image revealed pseudographitic domains with an interplanar distance ≈&nbsp;0.37–0.39 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>, Supporting Information). The specific surface area of the used hard carbon was 4.18 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, with an average pore volume of 0.0021 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The resulting electrode performance of the hard carbon electrode is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0001">1</a>. The first discharge (sodiation) led to delivery of a capacity of ≈355 mAh&nbsp;(g-hard carbon)<sup>−1</sup>, with an irreversible loss of ≈30&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> during the first charge (oxidation). The irreversibility at the first cycle is ascribed to the formation of the SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0032_R_d72541000e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">32</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0033_R_d72541000e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Evidence of this behavior is clearly observed in the first cycle plateau (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0001">1a</a>) or the cathodic peak on the cyclic voltammogram (CV) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a–c</a>, Supporting Information). The discharge–charge curve consists of two regions: a slope part until ≈100–120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a low-voltage plateau down to 0.005&nbsp;V even after 50 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0001">1a,b</a>). Under cyclic voltammetry conditions, the same two regions present current peaks between 0.2&nbsp;and 1.3&nbsp;V and a sharp current increase below 0.2&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The further cycling demonstrated better Coulombic efficiency (CE) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0001">1d</a>); however, capacity fading prevailed over cycling that would be associated with continuous sodium loss and growth of the SEI layer. The SEI layer growth most likely occurs during reduction at low potential which is why CE does not reach 100% in the whole cycling range. The highlighted view of d<i>Q</i>/d<i>U</i> presents a pair of peaks below 0.05&nbsp;V for discharge and at ≈0.12&nbsp;V for charge (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a>, Supporting Information). The analysis of the differential capacity evolution demonstrates that capacity loss is observed mainly in the low-voltage plateau region while the higher-voltage region maintains its capacity over cycling.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304300-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/45e4a020-8e77-4acf-b46f-b9f2e3bdafb3/aenm202304300-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/45e4a020-8e77-4acf-b46f-b9f2e3bdafb3/aenm202304300-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/55378e0d-1861-420b-8cc5-a3e8f6056202/aenm202304300-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/45e4a020-8e77-4acf-b46f-b9f2e3bdafb3/aenm202304300-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304300-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304300" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Galvanostatic voltage profiles of Na||Hard carbon cells for a) 1st cycle and b) 50th cycle, c) CV at 0.01&nbsp;mV/s with highlighted high voltage region, d) cyclability of hard carbon-sodium cell at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> plotted with CE = (Q<sup>+</sup>/Q<sup>−</sup>)∙100%. Electrolyte is 0.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in PC/FEC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>We monitored the two voltage regions using in situ Raman spectroscopy for fresh and extensive cycles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The survey spectra showed two distinct broad bands, D and G (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2b</a>), typically observed for carbon materials with a significant portion of graphitic ordering or graphene-like fragments and peaks of propylene carbonate solvent.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304300-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ba5866f7-2ec0-47c1-ae74-74f6c23f95be/aenm202304300-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ba5866f7-2ec0-47c1-ae74-74f6c23f95be/aenm202304300-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d4c53e2f-e4d4-4863-b8cb-e698fb1d517c/aenm202304300-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ba5866f7-2ec0-47c1-ae74-74f6c23f95be/aenm202304300-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304300-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304300" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">in situ Raman measurements of hard carbon electrodes. a) Scheme of in situ Raman measurements, b) survey spectra of hard carbon electrode in pristine (open circuit voltage - OCV) state and fully sodiated state, c) background-corrected spectra in low frequency range, reference lines are of sodium clusters stabilized in Ar matrix<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0035" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0035_R_d72541000e679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Inorg. Chem.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>: black – Na<sub>4</sub>, orange – Na<sub>8</sub>, inset shows possible structure of Na<sub>8</sub><sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0035" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0035_R_d72541000e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Inorg. Chem.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>; d,f,h) are normalized averaged in situ Raman spectra in carbon D and G band region upon (de)sodiation for the 1st, 10th, and 50th cycles correspondingly; e,g,i) are measured G-band peak position overlapped with corresponding (de)sodiation capacity. Each data point was obtained by processing at least 5 surface spot spectra.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In addition, we monitored the low-frequency spectral range to track the possibility of sodium-cluster formation as metal–metal bonds usually manifest themselves at low vibration energies. For instance, lithium, sodium, and potassium clusters isolated in an argon matrix produce signals at 200–600, 70–280, and 30–180&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0034_R_d72541000e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Chem. Phys.">34</a>-<a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0036_R_d72541000e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, we were able to see the emergence of a notable signal at low frequencies at 0.1&nbsp;V, and the signal was even stronger when the cell was fully sodiated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The most prominent peaks were observed at 112 and 132&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. The latter is close to the strongest signal of the magic cluster Na<sub>8</sub> isolated at 15 K in the Ar matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0035_R_d72541000e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Inorg. Chem.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Note that the room temperature and effect of carbon pores on cluster formation may lead to the formation of clusters of different structure or composition. It is noted that carbon pores would affect sodium cluster formation in comparison to isolated clusters. The low-frequency Raman signal suggests small cluster formation, likely Na<sub>4</sub> or Na<sub>8</sub>. In addition, Na<sub>4</sub> clusters can be stable on the defective graphene as theoretically predicted.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0037_R_d72541000e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Npj Comput.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The expected size of such clusters was proposed to be below 15 Å in diameter by means of pair distribution function analysis (PDF) and NMR studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0024_R_d72541000e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The small cluster size in closed pores was also investigated by a small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) and X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) spectroscopy by Iglesias et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0038_R_d72541000e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To the best of our knowledge, our work demonstrates the first Raman spectroscopy observation of sodium-cluster formation in hard carbon. In addition, we observed a low signal in the mentioned frequency range even at full desodiation at 2&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2c</a>), indicating the part of the irreversible capacity is likely associated with some irreversibly trapped sodium clusters.</p>
         
         <p>The D and G bands in non-cycled hard carbon were located at 1340&nbsp;and 1605 cm<sup>−1</sup>. As the hard carbon electrode was fully sodiated to 5&nbsp;mV, the corresponding G-band maximum shifted to 1560−1565 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2d–i</a>). At the same time, it is interesting to note no shift of the D band and its further broadening during 50 cycles. Compared to the discharge–charge curves, the position variation of the G band resembles the voltage profile. Specifically, as the sodiation progressed in the sloping region, the corresponding G band shifted in the negative direction (Δ&nbsp; = &nbsp;30–38 cm<sup>−1</sup>), below which a slight movement of the G band position (Δ&nbsp;=&nbsp;2–10&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) was observed on the flat plateau (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2d–i</a>). The observed phenomena were reversible during de/sodiation.</p>
         
         <p>Reddy et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0020_R_d72541000e769" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Energy Lett.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported the shift of the G band in the slope region and retention of the G-band position upon further sodiation. However, the recovery was limited during desodiation. Weaving et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0021_R_d72541000e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported reversible and persistent movement of the G-band in the sloping region. Given that the rise of the background slope at the first cycle was attributed to SEI fluorescence, as suggested by Weaving et&nbsp;al.,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0021_R_d72541000e779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we observed a similar tendency even after the extensive cycles on sodiation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2d,f,h</a>), particularly in the low-voltage region and more distinctly after 10 and 50 cycles (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). From this viewpoint, it is likely that such background signal is associated not only with SEI fluorescence but also with fluorescence of sodium clusters reversibly formed in nanopores at the low-voltage plateau. In addition to the background effect, we noticed a gradual change of the propylene carbonate (PC) peaks at 1124, 1150, and 1210 cm<sup>−1</sup> toward suppression as cycling progressed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2d,f,h</a>). Instead, a broad band between 1100 and 1230 cm<sup>−1</sup> appeared with no evident PC peaks. We explain its appearance by the gradual growth of the SEI layer, which usually contains Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, organic carbonate (PC in our case), and its decomposition products,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0014_R_d72541000e806" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Front. Chem.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> of which the overlapped signals would result in a broad band in that frequency region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0039_R_d72541000e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Spectrochim. Acta A">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Again, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), the reversible G-band shift upon de/sodiation demonstrates that the slope region (&gt;&nbsp;0.1&nbsp;V) is related to sodium intercalation into pseudo-graphitic domains, as neither adsorption nor pore filling could not affect the vibration pattern to such a high extent. The electrons in this case likely localize at π*-antibonding carbon orbitals, as proposed in earlier work on PDF analysis and solid-state NMR.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0024_R_d72541000e826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The charge transfer causes increase of C─C bond length and consequent G-band shift. The DFT calculations support the G-band shift for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation between graphitic layers up to roughly NaC<sub>24</sub> and the stable position of the D band in the sloping region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0020_R_d72541000e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Energy Lett.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The minor G-peak shift observed in our work below ≈0.1&nbsp;V (at sodiation extent above ≈120&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) likely demonstrates that potential ranges of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and pore filling slightly overlap at ≈0.05–0.15&nbsp;V.</p>
         
         <p>To determine how intercalation and pore filling affect the charge-transfer kinetics, sodium||hard carbon cells were analyzed using EIS during de/sodiation. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0003">3a</a> shows an electrical equivalent circuit for the EIS spectra fitting after the first (de)sodiation. To minimize the ratio of the Na electrode impedance to hard carbon electrode impedance and minimize sodium polarization, we used small hard-carbon electrodes for EIS (<i>d</i> ≈ 3&nbsp;mm). The impedance of hard carbon thus appears relatively high, which was done deliberately for convenience of analysis. The equivalent circuit contains the ohmic resistance&nbsp;<i>R</i><sub>u</sub>, primarily representing that of the electrolyte, the impedance of the Na electrode, and the impedance of the hard-carbon electrode. The hard-carbon impedance was approximated by <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>–<i>C</i><sub>p</sub>–<i>Z</i><sub>W</sub>/<i>Z</i><sub>dl</sub> elements connected in series with a <i>R</i>’/<i>Z</i>’ block. The <i>Z</i><sub>dl</sub> is the double-layer impedance approximated by the constant phase element (CPE) representing deviations from an ideal capacitor.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0040_R_d72541000e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <i>R</i><sub>ct,HC</sub> represents the interfacial charge-transfer resistance connected in series with the Warburg impedance, <i>Z</i><sub>W</sub>, and capacitance element, <i>C</i><sub>p.</sub> The physical meaning of the latter capacitance element, <i>C</i><sub>p</sub>, can be considered as the reversible “intercalation capacity”; previously, it was shown valid in this regard by Levi and Aurbach for Li<sup>+</sup> intercalation in graphite<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0041_R_d72541000e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Electrochim. Acta">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and in LiNiO<sub>2.</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0042_R_d72541000e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Electrochim. Acta">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This combination of diffusion impedance in series with the capacitance element, in general, is referred to as the Frumkin and Melik–Gaikazyan impedance and satisfactory describes intercalation in battery electrodes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0041_R_d72541000e914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Electrochim. Acta">41</a>-<a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0043_R_d72541000e914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> although it was originally proposed for the description of adsorption impedance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0044_R_d72541000e919" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Dokl. Akad. Nauk SSSR">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304300-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6f875a99-ac0a-4fd9-90cb-73d732c48658/aenm202304300-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6f875a99-ac0a-4fd9-90cb-73d732c48658/aenm202304300-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c5e8ca2e-d1cf-4f99-9bac-656623d58ec6/aenm202304300-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6f875a99-ac0a-4fd9-90cb-73d732c48658/aenm202304300-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304300-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304300" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) EIS spectrum of fully sodiated (reduced at 10&nbsp;mV) hard carbon with fitted curve and corresponding equivalent electric circuit; b) Selected spectra obtained at different cell voltage. The spectra were artificially shifted along the vertical axis for easier comparison. Hard carbon electrode diameter was ≈ 3&nbsp;mm and hard carbon mass 0.15&nbsp;mg.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The combination of semi-infinite linear diffusion impedance with&nbsp;<i>C</i><sub>p</sub> showed better fitting than the finite-space Warburg element, which is most likely a result of the non-uniform particle size distribution and high disorder of the real electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0045_R_d72541000e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The addition of <i>R</i>’/<i>CPE</i>’ impedance to the equivalent circuit was required for a satisfactory fit of the low-voltage mid-frequency range of cycled electrodes. As will be discussed later, it most likely originated from the SEI layer growth.</p>
         
         <p>As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0003">3b</a>, the equivalent circuit satisfactorily fits the spectra at different states of sodiation. No drastic changes in impedance character, such as the appearance or disappearance of new features, were observed within the single cycle. The main noticeable difference is the decrease of the charge-transfer resistance of the <i>hard carbon</i>&nbsp;|&nbsp;<i>electrolyte</i> interface at lower voltages. To understand this behavior comprehensively, we analyzed spectra at various voltages in both scan directions for the fresh (1st cycle) and cycled (10th and 50th cycles) states using the same electrode (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0004">4</a>). The prolonged cycling resulted in increased interfacial impedance both for the charged (desodiated) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0004">4a</a>) and discharged (sodiated) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0004">4b</a>) states of hard carbon. Along with <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> growth upon multiple de/sodiation, the repeated cycling resulted in changes of the spectra character in the mid-frequency range (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Although the desodiated hard carbon showed a shift on the Bode plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0004">4a</a>, inset) toward lower frequency, as expected for increased <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value, the sodiated hard carbon demonstrated a new peak component on the Bode plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0004">4b</a>, inset). The abovementioned <i>R</i>’/<i>Z</i>’ impedance was introduced to the equivalent circuit to fit that component. We associate this additional impedance feature with a growing SEI layer, as it introduces an additional charge-transfer step (Na<sup>+</sup><sub>electrolyte</sub>→ Na<sup>+</sup><sub>SEI</sub> → Na<sup>+</sup><sub>carbon</sub>). The emergence of the additional, SEI-associated charge-transfer resistance is typical for SEI impedance phenomena.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0027_R_d72541000e1010" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">27</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0046_R_d72541000e1010" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Technol.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304300-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1b3d69f0-8921-46e5-99e8-f8e558cb5bff/aenm202304300-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1b3d69f0-8921-46e5-99e8-f8e558cb5bff/aenm202304300-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5a880c6d-9a0b-4be5-87dd-c491dc067abd/aenm202304300-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1b3d69f0-8921-46e5-99e8-f8e558cb5bff/aenm202304300-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304300-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304300" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Results of EIS fitting for Na||Hard carbon cell. a) Raw and fitted spectra at 0.6&nbsp;V; b) raw and fitted spectra at 0.01&nbsp;V; c) cyclic voltammetry and current estimated for intercalation capacitance in zero-rate approximation; e) 10th galvanostatic (dis)charge curve; Reciprocal charge transfer resistance plotted versus d) voltage and f) capacity (for convenience of comparison, 1, 10, and 50 plots were adjusted to same state of (dis)charge).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further understand the growth of the interfacial charge-transfer resistance and changes in the charge-transfer kinetics, we fitted all the spectra and presented <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <i>C</i><sub>p</sub> values versus voltage and specific capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0004">4c–f</a>). As mentioned above, lowering the voltage leads to a decrease in the charge-transfer resistance, facilitating electrochemical reaction at more sodiated carbon. The most prominent decrease in the charge-transfer resistance (increase of its reciprocal value, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub><sup>−1</sup>) follows the range between 1.0&nbsp;and 0.2&nbsp;V. That change is especially pronounced when plotted relative to the de/sodiation capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0004">4f</a>). The substantial kinetics enhancement proceeds during the sloping region of the voltage profile; then, only minor changes in the charge-transfer resistance are observed when more than half of the capacity is delivered in the low plateau range. This distinct correlation with the voltage profile and interfacial charge-transfer resistance stability highlights almost no interfacial changes in the low-voltage plateau part. This behavior is interpreted to be pore filling at the low-voltage plateau, whereas intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> is predominant on the sloping part. From the above findings, the pore filling should be considered as small-cluster formation in “closed” pores without electrolyte contact with a pore, as the open-pore process would have more influence on interfacial impedance or changing the character of impedance dramatically. The closed rather than open pore filling is supported in recent works, where a larger plateau capacity is attributed to a higher closed-pore fraction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0038_R_d72541000e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0047_R_d72541000e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Batter. Supercaps">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The double-layer capacitance, <i>C</i><sub>dl</sub>, did not show drastic changes in the plateau part (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a–g</a>, Supporting Information), which supports the mentioned closed pore filling rather than open-pore process. In contrast, <i>C</i><sub>dl</sub> varies much more visibly in the higher voltage region, especially upon desodiation. The strong irreversible rise of <i>C</i><sub>dl</sub> upon the first cycle is likely to be associated with the SEI formation and irreversible capacity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8e</a>, Supporting Information). Interestingly, such a strong hysteresis prevails even at the 50th cycle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8g</a>, Supporting Information). This behavior cannot be easily explained by polarization as the EIS spectra were recorded under steady-state conditions. Instead, we suggest that the <i>C</i><sub>dl</sub> hysteresis originates from intrinsic irreversibility from dis/ordering transition that was previously proposed to describe results on in situ XRD of hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0048_R_d72541000e1084" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0049_R_d72541000e1084" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Mater. Chem. A Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such irreversibility also manifests itself in the low-scan-rate cyclic voltammogram asymmetry (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0001">1</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Thus, strong changes of Raman patterns, charge-transfer resistance, and double-layer capacitance in the sloping region justify Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation in pseudo-graphitic domains accompanied with dis/ordering transition.</p>
         
         <p>The analysis of <i>C</i><sub>p</sub> as the intercalation capacity showed slightly unexpected results. The estimation of direct current from <i>C</i><sub>p</sub> under resistance-free conditions <i>I</i> = <i>C</i><sub>p</sub> (d<i>E</i>/d<i>t</i>) gave comparable results of experimental slow-scan-rate CV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0004">4c</a>). More interestingly, despite some data fluctuation due to experimental and fitting errors, the intercalation capacity did not show a systematic tendency of fading, which is opposite to the capacity fading observed during discharge (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0001">1</a>).&nbsp;This result implies that capacity fading is dominantly determined by interfacial resistance growth. That growth has especially dramatic consequences in the low-voltage plateau region as the slowed kinetics would shift the pore sodiation potential below the negative voltage limit, resulting in part of the capacity being inaccessible. This finding agrees with the differential capacity analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information); namely, there was no capacity fading tendency in the high-voltage region, whereas the clear peak separation emerges for the low-voltage process after the extensive cycling.</p>
         
         <p>We could not count the <i>C</i><sub>p</sub>-element-related capacitance (interpreted as intercalation capacity) as pseudocapacitive in this work, as it is coupled with pronounced charge-transfer resistance and diffusion impedance at any voltage. The Nyquist plot demonstrates only leaning toward capacitive behavior even at low frequencies but does not entirely resemble a vertical line, as is expected when the capacitive regime is achieved, as, for instance, the case for an activated carbon with a clear adsorption-style behavior (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, although a linear dependency of current on the scan rate may be observed in some potential range, as we observed in our work (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) or literature,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0019_R_d72541000e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Electrochim. Acta">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> only this hint of pseudocapacitive behavior in a limited time domain should not be directly considered as evidence of the adsorption mechanism.</p>
         
         <p>To demonstrate the consequence of charge-transfer growth on dis/charge kinetics, we estimated an analog of “<i>RC</i>-constant” for the de/sodiation process in the entire voltage range as a product of the charge-transfer resistance <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <i>C</i><sub>p</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The RC value was estimated to be ≈400&nbsp;s for the middle of the sloping part and ≈2000 s for the plateau. After 50 cycles, it approximately doubled, leading to too high RC values (≈ 4000 s) at low voltage. This finding demonstrates that the kinetics is too slow to provide complete pore filling even at low currents or scan rates after an increase in the interfacial resistance. Therefore, it should be noted that such RC analysis ignores the diffusion limitation; however, as will be discussed later, the diffusivity demonstrates a similar trend.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element"><b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0005">5</a> presents the estimation of the apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in hard carbon from the GITT and EIS data. We note that <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> here should be considered as apparent values because the exact electroactive surface area is not known (the BET-derived area was used as an approximation) and Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and pore filling are inherently different processes and apparently address different reaction interfaces. The GITT-derived apparent diffusion coefficients were calculated using formula<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0050_R_d72541000e1174" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Electrochem. Soc">50</a>-<a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0052_R_d72541000e1174" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (1):
