The diffusion factor in GITT is calculated as the slope d<i>E</i>/d<i>t</i><sup>1/2</sup>, with similar information contained in the Warburg coefficient (<i>A</i><sub>W</sub>) acquired from AC data.</div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304300-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f8d20cc9-ea27-4625-a8dc-f6a98822ce16/aenm202304300-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f8d20cc9-ea27-4625-a8dc-f6a98822ce16/aenm202304300-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b6330076-f4e4-4209-a8c4-49ee54fab6d9/aenm202304300-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f8d20cc9-ea27-4625-a8dc-f6a98822ce16/aenm202304300-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304300-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304300" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Apparent diffusion coefficient of Na in hard carbon. a–c) GITT voltage profiles (pulse-relaxation) for 1st, 10th, and 50th cycles, d–f) apparent <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> estimated via GITT and EIS versus capacity plot, g) and (h) are GITT-derived and EIS-derived apparent <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> normalized for the state of (dis)charge to account for capacity fade.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The estimated diffusion coefficient values range from 10<sup>−14</sup> to 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. Both the EIS- and GITT-derived diffusion data present similar trends, although the EIS values are slightly higher than the GITT values. First, upon sodiation, the diffusion coefficient increases up to the inflection point of the voltage profile and then starts to fade as the slope region transforms into the plateau. When the plateau is established, the apparent diffusivity falls by more than an order of magnitude and remains at this level until nearly 10&nbsp;mV, at which point it strongly increases. Upon switching to desodiation, the opposite sequence occurs<i>: D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> decreases to low values at the plateau onset, increases when the plateau becomes a slope, and decreases again at the highly desodiated state. The low values of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> in the plateau region are attributed to the change from the intercalation to pore-filling process. Considering the pore filling as an inner-pore process (no electrolyte access), this difference may arise not from the intrinsic difference of&nbsp;<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> at different sodiation degrees but rather by dimension or surface area effect, as the <i>electrode</i>&nbsp;|&nbsp;<i>electrolyte</i> area is the reaction zone for the intercalation in the high-voltage region. In contrast, some <i>inner areas</i> around pores are activated for diffusion inside carbon while filling the pores with sodium clusters (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0006">6</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304300-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/740dbb0e-decd-4086-ac19-94d7e8f8c4e9/aenm202304300-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/740dbb0e-decd-4086-ac19-94d7e8f8c4e9/aenm202304300-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/26867962-214e-46eb-9c6d-8cc51d81579e/aenm202304300-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/740dbb0e-decd-4086-ac19-94d7e8f8c4e9/aenm202304300-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304300-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304300" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schema on diffusion in hard carbon particle for insertion into pseudo-graphitic domains and b) inside the carbon into the pores. Dashed red lines illustrate the diffusion front for intercalation and pore filling. The schema on the right demonstrates pore filling-cluster formation via solid-state sodium diffusion inside hard carbon particle.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The diffusion coefficient slightly decreases in the plateau range during cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0005">5g,h</a>). This decreasing tendency at the highly sodiated state is caused by the growth of the SEI layer, which directly affects the interfacial charge transfer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0004">4f</a>) and apparently sodium ion transport as well. The growing limitation associated with diffusion contributes to sluggish kinetics below 0.2&nbsp;V. The increase of the diffusion coefficient at the early stage of sodiation (vice versa on desodiation) above 0.5&nbsp;V is not clear at present. It is likely that hard carbon graphenic domains become accessible (active) as the resulting potential of hard carbon decreases gradually, which agrees with the growth of the double-layer capacitance upon the beginning of sodiation and its decrease at the last stages of desodiation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a>, Supporting Information). If more graphenic domains are involved in the reaction, the diffusion proceeds faster in a multi-domain disordered particle.</p>
         
         <p>To understand structural changes of hard carbon (4.18 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) electrode we applied <i>operando</i> XRD (<i>o</i>-XRD) upon the first cycle (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0007">7</a>). Given from the <i>o</i>-XRD data, the resulting (002) plane is gradually shifted toward lower angle (2θ) in the sloping region to ≈0.15&nbsp;V, as the sodiation (reduction) progresses (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0007">7a</a>). One interesting feature is that there was no shrinkage of the peak width in this process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0007">7b</a>) in contrast to literature data.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0012_R_d72541000e1474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0049_R_d72541000e1474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Mater. Chem. A Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further sodiation resulted in a less notable shift of the peak position in the plateau region to 0.005&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0007">7a</a>). On charge (oxidation), the movement of the peak was reversible; that is, a slow movement on the plateau region to ≈0.15&nbsp;V followed by notable shift on the sloping region by the end of charge. This feature highlights that the de/sodiation in the sloping region was mainly contributed by de/intercalation of sodium ions into/out of the hard carbon with the small specific surface area in this work. On the other hand, in a work using hard carbon with a larger specific surface area (≈24 and ≈82 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0012_R_d72541000e1487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the width of (002) Bragg peak was narrowed and remained at the same peak position on the sloping region, which was proposed as an adsorption process. Further sodiation of the hard carbon induced the peak slight shift toward lower angle as a result of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion reaction although the extent was not so notable. Such a difference with some literature data may be caused by portion of specific surface area in charge storage along with graphenic domain/pore distribution.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304300-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/88297c47-5a12-4a2f-b0b2-f4d299cdac13/aenm202304300-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/88297c47-5a12-4a2f-b0b2-f4d299cdac13/aenm202304300-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5717c1ca-5624-4f2b-bcfa-b9ade9ba9bb3/aenm202304300-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/88297c47-5a12-4a2f-b0b2-f4d299cdac13/aenm202304300-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304300-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304300" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) <i>Operando</i> XRD patterns of hard carbon electrode upon (de)sodiation and b) corresponding selected XRD patterns for initial, sodiated, and desodiated state.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">While a traditional structure investigation method like XRD might have some limitation toward study of such a disordered material as hard carbon, we focused more on obtaining fundamental information via EIS. Given the temperature dependence of the EIS response, the activation energy can be derived by performing EIS between 20 and 40 °C at various states of charge (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0008">8</a>). The charge-transfer resistance decreases with increasing temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0008">8a</a>), and its temperature dependence obeys the Arrhenius activation law (3), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0008">8b</a>:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202304300-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/3dc0e062-7f26-449a-aaa6-901e9ae1fc0f/aenm202304300-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202304300:aenm202304300-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202304300-math-0003.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msubsup>
                              <mi>R</mi>
                              <mi>ct</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mo>−</mo>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                              </mrow>
                           </msubsup>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>A</mi>
                              <mn>0</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mo>−</mo>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mi>a</mi>
                                 <mo>/</mo>
                                 <mi>R</mi>
                                 <mi>T</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{R}_{{\mathrm{ct}}}^{ - 1} = {A}_0{{\mathrm{e}}}^{ - Ea/RT}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304300-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/809bac39-26b2-4eac-9bbc-0d6841c2a69c/aenm202304300-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/809bac39-26b2-4eac-9bbc-0d6841c2a69c/aenm202304300-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/685c1039-f510-4243-baaf-27114f0156a1/aenm202304300-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/809bac39-26b2-4eac-9bbc-0d6841c2a69c/aenm202304300-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304300-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304300" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) EIS spectra of Na|NaPF<sub>6</sub>|HC cell at 0.4&nbsp;V under various temperatures, b) charge-transfer-derived Arrhenius plot, and c) activation energy of charge transfer process versus specific capacity (after 50 cycles).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Here, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> is the charge-transfer resistance, <i>A</i><sub>0</sub> is the pre-exponential constant, <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> is the activation energy, <i>R</i> is the universal gas constant, and <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature. As a result, the activation energy is estimated to be the slope of the ln <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub><sup>−1</sup> – 1/<i>T</i> curve. The resulting <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) values are ≈&nbsp;60–70&nbsp;kJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup> for the desodiated state and decrease to 50–60 kJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup> for sodiated carbon. In general, a measured activation energy of charge transfer is a value with contributions of various steps accompanying charge transfer at an interface (ion desolvation, solvent re-organization, ion adsorption, etc.)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0054_R_d72541000e1616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Electrochim. Acta">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Depending on the particular limiting step, some of these processes (e.g., desolvation) have a major contribution to the net value. In comparison with Li-ion systems, the range of our results fits charge-transfer activation energies for the graphite|LiPF<sub>6</sub> in the EC/DMC interface (57–71&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0055_R_d72541000e1626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and graphene-like graphite in LiClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DMC (53–56&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0056_R_d72541000e1636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - J. Phys. Chem. C">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The relatively high activation energy values close to those of Li in graphite systems indicates the effect of desolvation energy on the overall electrochemical process at the interface between hard carbon and the electrolyte. The potential dependence (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0008">8c</a>) of the activation energy represents a conventional understanding of electrochemical reaction according to Butler–Volmer theory when the electrode potential change affects the Gibbs free energy change of reaction and activation energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0057_R_d72541000e1644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To further check to what extent hard carbon behavior resembles intercalation or adsorption, we compared our data with literature activation energy for various processes (<b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#aenm202304300-tbl-0001">1</a>). The typical adsorption activation energy does not exceed ≈30&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, according to literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0058_R_d72541000e1658" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">58</a>-<a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0060_R_d72541000e1658" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Power Sources">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the case of adsorption on amorphous and nano-sized carbons (carbon nanotubes - CNT, graphene, and activated carbon electrodes), the activation energy is even lower than 5&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0059_R_d72541000e1666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Carbon">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Indeed, ion adsorption usually requires rearrangements or reorientation of ions and solvent molecules at the interface, while this process is much easier than full desolvation and ion transfer from liquid to solid phase. Instead, as mentioned above, our experimental data (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub> = 53–69&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) show similar values to Li<sup>+</sup> apparent charge transfer activation energy at graphite/organic carbonate interface – upon lithium intercalation in graphite, <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> = 57–71&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0055_R_d72541000e1684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the sloping (de)sodiation region is most likely governed by the intercalation of desolvated sodium ions rather than ion adsorption at the interface</p>

            <div class="article-section__table-footnotes">
               <ul>

         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Generally, ion diffusion is also temperature-dependent. For solid-state diffusion, it may obey activation-type behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0061_R_d72541000e1874" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - J. Power Sources">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this case, the diffusion coefficient depends on temperature similarly to formula (3):
            <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202304300-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a8e0ffcc-3624-4a30-a38c-3290efe1c410/aenm202304300-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202304300:aenm202304300-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202304300-math-0004.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>D</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mn>0</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mo>−</mo>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mi>a</mi>
                                 <mo>/</mo>
                                 <mi>R</mi>
                                 <mi>T</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}D = {D}_0{{\mathrm{e}}}^{ - Ea/RT}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The diffusion activation energy, <i>E</i><sub>a,D</sub>, can be obtained from the EIS-measured Warburg coefficient, <i>A</i><sub>w</sub>, as the latter is proportional to <i>D</i><sup>−2</sup> (2). Consequently, the activation energy of diffusion can be derived using formula (5):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202304300-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/cdffe7d8-3151-4812-a5da-5fefd48417cb/aenm202304300-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202304300:aenm202304300-math-0005" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202304300-math-0005.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>ln</mi>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>A</mi>
                              <mi>W</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mfrac>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>a</mi>
                                    <mo>,</mo>
                                    <mi>D</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msub>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                                 <mi>RT</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfrac>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <mi>const</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} \ln \ {A}_{W} = \frac{{E}_{a,D}}{2\textit{RT}} + \mathrm{const} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>The determined <i>E</i><sub>a,D</sub> values are considerably scattered between 50 and 90&nbsp;kJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup> with no clear dependence on potential (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Such disruption may arise from the possibility of experimental error in the case of <i>A</i><sub>W</sub> determination, as diffusion impedance likely deviates from pure linear semi-infinite diffusion due to the highly disordered hard-carbon nature. The values are much higher than those measured via positive muon spin rotation and relaxation (39(7) meV or 3.8(3) kJ mol<sup>−1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0062_R_d72541000e1959" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - ACS Phys. Chem. Au">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 88 meV or 8.5&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0063_R_d72541000e1964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Mater. Chem. A Mater.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>). It is thought that the difference between the two methods originates from the different diffusion types measured.</p>
         
         <p>Muon spectroscopy likely gives values of 2D diffusion, i.e., Na<sup>+</sup> moving along a 2D graphenic layer in a graphene-like domain.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0062_R_d72541000e1974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - ACS Phys. Chem. Au">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the EIS-derived diffusion coefficient results in an average apparent value of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the whole electrochemically active particle. Such diffusion would inevitably include inter-domain (3D) Na<sup>+</sup> transport, and we expect that such transport of sodium ions requires much more energy to be activated and hence acts as a limiting step in Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion inside the hard carbon particle. We assume that distinguishing the activation energy for intercalation and pore filling requires a different diffusion model used in EIS and additional measurements with a broader frequency range and higher number of frequencies. In general, the measured activation energy for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in hard carbon particle is within range of apparent values for Li/graphite or K/graphite systems (0.2–1.3&nbsp;eV depending on the method, conditions, and phase composition)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0064_R_d72541000e1988" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Phys. Rev. B">64</a>-<a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0067_R_d72541000e1988" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - J. Power Sources">67</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which again demonstrates similarity of hard carbon to a classical intercalation host rather than adsorptive electrode.</p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0009">9</a> summarizes the hard-carbon behavior in sodium cells studied via in situ Raman spectroscopy, GITT, EIS, and TEM. In general, in situ Raman and EIS results resemble the voltage profile in their two-step appearance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0009">9a,b</a>). The carbon G-band peak shifts reversibly at relatively high voltage, indicating sodium intercalation in pseudo-graphitic domains. In contrast, the G-band position does not change considerably during the low-voltage plateau. Instead, prominent signals emerge in the Raman spectra below 150 cm<sup>−1</sup> at low voltage, supporting the formation of small sodium clusters.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304300-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/33a9d541-8f32-41f1-8ea3-d90bcadaea15/aenm202304300-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/33a9d541-8f32-41f1-8ea3-d90bcadaea15/aenm202304300-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8546dce2-09fb-4b43-a93b-f5b14c5e903d/aenm202304300-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/33a9d541-8f32-41f1-8ea3-d90bcadaea15/aenm202304300-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304300-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304300" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Summary of different processes of Na storage in hard carbon revealed&nbsp;via&nbsp;in situ&nbsp;Raman and EIS, b) Inserted sodium ions (dark blue) and pore-occupied Na clusters (light blue) as primary contributors to charge storage in hard carbon, c) TEM image of SEI layer grown on the hard carbon surface after 50 (de)sodiation cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The charge-transfer resistance gradually decreases when sodium is intercalated but stabilizes on the plateau region. The apparent diffusion coefficient reacts to a process change and shrinks by approximately an order of magnitude when pore filling follows intercalation, representing Na dynamics in and around pores. The limited variation of the charge-transfer resistance, double-layer capacitance, and carbon-related Raman signal on the low-voltage plateau suggest that the filling process is mostly associated with closed pores, which have no contact with the electrolyte rather than an open-pore process. These results lead to the conclusion that Na<sup>+</sup> ions must diffuse through the solid to reach the pores. Therefore, the capacity of hard-carbon electrodes fades when they are tested at high rates<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0011_R_d72541000e2029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0026_R_d72541000e2029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0068_R_d72541000e2029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Electrochemistry">68</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as a result of diffusion- and kinetic-limited behavior in this low-voltage plateau region. Also, some irreversibly trapped sodium clusters may cause minor capacity loss at the first cycle as mentioned in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2c</a>.</p>
         
         <p>Our findings above do not include the previously mentioned three consecutive stages of sodium storage, where sizeable Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption is regarded as a contributor to a specific capacity. We observed only limited double-layer behavior in parallel with intercalation/pore-filling capacity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Adsorption occurs; however, we were not able to count it as a comparable contributor with insertion or pore filling in our work. However, the capacitive contribution is more likely to be available depending on the nature of hard carbons, as the specific surface area, amount of defect, surface composition, and presence of heteroatom strongly depend on the conditions of the hard carbon synthesis method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0010_R_d72541000e2049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Mater. Today">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A relatively low BET-area (4.18 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) and the resulting large particle size (8–12&nbsp;µm) in our samples shows that hard carbon can function by relying on intercalation and closed pore filling. Although, it is natural that if hard carbon area available for electrolyte penetration reaches hundreds of m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, the contribution of adsorption would be significant taking into account the overall mechanism consideration.</p>
         
         <p>The prevailing of the cathodic over anodic process on a HC electrode is a result of irreversible reactions, which contribute to SEI growth during sodiation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0009">9c</a>). The SEI growth is observed both in a direct way after prolonged cycling via TEM when its thickness reaches 10–30&nbsp;nm and in an indirect way as a gradual rise of interfacial charge-transfer resistance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0004">4</a>), diffusivity suppression (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0005">5</a>), and solvent-associated Raman signal changes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304300-fig-0002">2d–f</a>, 1100–1250&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>). The growth of the SEI layer and corresponding interfacial resistance increase have a decisive influence on the de/sodiation kinetics in the low-voltage plateau region, impeding charge–discharge and lowering specific capacity. In this regard, SEI and hard-carbon surface engineering are believed to be key parameters to be optimized for stabilizing the performance for stable Na-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202304300-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202304300-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, we comprehensively examined the mechanism of sodium storage in hard carbon regarding de/sodiation kinetics via in situ Raman, EIS, and GITT analyses during cycling. The in situ Raman spectra at low voltage demonstrated the formation of small sodium clusters in closed pores. We observed the reversible shift of the carbon G-band above 0.1&nbsp;V, which was accompanied by a strong change of the interfacial charge-transfer resistance and moderate sodium diffusion rate that indicates Na (de)intercalation behavior. In contrast, the low-voltage plateau showed a minor change of the G-band maximum and interfacial resistance but a prominent decrease of diffusivity, representing a transition from intercalation to inner pore filling. Analysis of the EIS data demonstrated no significant capacitive contribution to the specific capacity. The gradual growth of the SEI layer upon multiple de/sodiation leads to an increase of the interfacial impedance that eventually delays full sodiation at low voltage together with some irreversibly trapped sodium clusters and hence causes capacity fade.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202304300-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202304300-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202304300-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202304300-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202304300-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202304300-sec-0070-title"> Electrodes and Cells Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>Commercial hard carbon (Beyond battery, Kuranode from Kuraray, Type 2, 9&nbsp;µm) powder was heated under 700&nbsp; °C for 4&nbsp;h in Ar prior to use. The electrodes were prepared with 93&nbsp;wt.% hard carbon and 7&nbsp;wt.% carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder. The mixture was deliberately made without other carbons to avoid interference from conductive additive (de)sodiation. The obtained slurry was applied onto Cu foil using a doctor blade. The resulting mass loading was ≈2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. For in situ Raman, perforated Cu-foil electrodes were prepared because experiment geometry required flipped electrode configuration. The diameter of electrodes was 10&nbsp;mm for cyclability and Raman cells, while 3&nbsp;mm for EIS and GITT to minimize counter/working impedance ratio that allowed us easily to distinguish hard carbon and sodium electrodes in AC response and minimize sodium electrode polarization. The used electrolyte was a commercial 0.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) and fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) in a volume ratio of 98:2. Sodium metal discs (16&nbsp;mm in diameter) were used as counter electrodes with a glass fiber separator in R2032 type coin cells. Coin cells with a thin glass window-modified case were used for in situ Raman spectroscopy.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202304300-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202304300-sec-0080-title"> Material Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>In situ Raman spectra were recorded on a&nbsp;Renishaw&nbsp;InVia Raman spectrometer equipped with a 633&nbsp;nm laser source. In situ Raman spectra below 300&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> were obtained using a 532&nbsp;nm laser. Jeol JEM-ARM200F scanning transmission electron microscope was employed to identify solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation on extensively cycled hard carbon electrode. BELSORP-max specific surface area analyzer was used for hard carbon specific area estimation using BET method. The BET area was 4.18&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. XRD patterns were obtained using PANalytical X'Pert diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.5406 Å). A stainless steel-teflon cell equipped with a beryllium window was used for <i>operando XRD</i> measurements.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202304300-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202304300-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>Galvanostatic charge–discharge at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> was utilized for cyclability tests. Cyclic voltammetry was performed at different scan rates between 0.005 and 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Electrochemical impedance spectra were recorded each 100&nbsp;mV starting from&nbsp;U&nbsp; = 1.2&nbsp;V till&nbsp;U&nbsp; = 0.2&nbsp;V and each 50&nbsp;mV between 0.01 and 0.2&nbsp;V using BioLogic VMP 3 potentiostat. Conditioning time was 2&nbsp;h for steps above 0.2&nbsp;V and 4&nbsp;h for steps below 0.2&nbsp;V. EIS data were recorded between 200&nbsp;kHz and 0.4 mHz with a 4&nbsp;mV amplitude. A relatively low amplitude was chosen due to the high slope of the voltammogram in the low voltage region. Spectra were analyzed using EISSA softaware.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304300-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304300-bib-0069_R_d72541000e2142" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69">69</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> GITT-derived diffusion coefficient was calculated using linear part of <i>I</i>-<i>t</i><sup>0.5</sup> curve, related to first 100&nbsp;s of current pulse. The EIS-derived diffusion coefficient was estimated from <i>A</i><sub>w</sub> parameter by fitting. The deviation from semi-infinite diffusion condition and particle size distribution resulted in slightly different values of diffusion coefficients obtained by two methods, although the calculated values showed a similar trend for both methods. Hard carbon in Raman cells was galvanostatically (de)sodiated at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> to a certain potential and held for at least 12&nbsp;h before Raman measurements. For the measurents, cells were disconnected. Voltage drift before and after Raman spectra acquisition did not exceed 15&nbsp;mV. Temperature for the most electrochemical tests was 25.0&nbsp;±&nbsp;0.5 °C. Activation energy measurements were recorded using BVT MT-1 thermostat. Temperature for activation energy determination was 25.0&nbsp;±&nbsp;0.1 °C. At least 2&nbsp;h conditioning time was used before each spectrum acquisition.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
